♠ Original name ♣ Monte Albán IIIB and IV ♥
♠ Alternative names ♣ Monte Albán IIIB; Monte Albán IVA; Late Classic ♥
♠ Peak Date ♣ ♥
♠ Duration ♣ 500 CE-900 CE ♥ “With the decline of Monte Albán around A.D. 700, the Zapotec entered into their fourth developmental stage: a period of highly competitive and militaristic “city states” which lasted until the Spanish conquest of the 1520s.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_1_MxAlb3B]
♠ Degree of centralization ♣ quasi-polity ♥ The Zapotec state began to fragment at the end of the IIIA period, and eventually formed numerous smaller competing "kingdoms", each politically independent of the others.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_2_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_3_MxAlb3B]
♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ none ♥ The Zapotec state began to fragment at the end of the IIIA period, and eventually formed numerous smaller competing "kingdoms", each politically independent of the others[MAJIDBENAM_REF_4_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_5_MxAlb3B] although there were marriage alliances between elites, as recorded in genealogical registers. Marriage alliances became increasingly important as a political tool through the IIIB to IV periods.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_6_MxAlb3B]
♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ MxAlb3A ♥
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ continuity ♥ The Zapotec polity went into gradual decline during this period, with population dispersal across the valley.The Zapotec state began to fragment at the end of the IIIA period, and eventually formed numerous smaller competing "kingdoms", each politically independent of the others.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_7_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_8_MxAlb3B]
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Zapotec, Monte Albán V ♥ The Zapotec people continued to exist, but no longer formed a state.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_9_MxAlb3B]
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ ♥
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ ♥
♠ Capital ♣ none ♥ As the population at Monte Alban declined, and the Zapotec state fragmented into smaller polities, a capital cannot be named for this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_10_MxAlb3B]
♠ Language ♣ Zapotec ♥ [MAJIDBENAM_REF_11_MxAlb3B]
♠ Linguistic Family ♣ Otomanguean ♥ Both the Zapotecan (Zapotec and Chatino) and Mixtecan (Mixtec, Cuicatec and Trique) languages belong to the Otomanguean language family, which also includes: Otopamean, Chinantecan Popolocan, Huave, Amuzgo and Manguean language groups.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_12_MxAlb3B]
♠ Language Genus ♣ ♥
♠ Religious Tradition ♣ Monte Albán Religion ♥ Monte Albán Religion. Besides royal ancestors, 39 gods have been identified, most of them identified with natural elements or entities (e.g. maize). Other natural elements or entities include saurian, snakes, jaguars.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_13_MxAlb3B].
♠ Religion Family ♣ ♥
♠ Religion Sect ♣ ♥General description[MAJIDBENAM_BR]During the Monte Alban IIIB and IV periods (500-900 CE) in the Valley of Oaxaca Monte Alban and the capital of the neighbouring state, Teotihuacaln declined, with public buildings no longer being maintained and the population of the capitals declining. A mixture of internal and external reasons for this decline are suggested, and has been summed up by Flannery and Marcus: ‘...without the ever-present competitive threat of Teotihuacan, there was one less reason to support what already had become a maladaptive concentration of population on an unproductive 400-m mountaintop.’[MAJIDBENAM_REF_14_MxAlb3B] Unfortunately, detailed chronological data from this period is not present as the ceramic sequences of the IIIB and IV phases are very difficult to differentiate and radiocarbon dates have yet to refine the sequence.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_15_MxAlb3B]The IIIb and IV phases have therefore been combined as one phase on this page. During this phase the population which had previously occupied the capital dispersed into smaller settlements throughout the valley and the valley became occupied by a series of militaristic ‘city states,[MAJIDBENAM_REF_16_MxAlb3B] which lasted until the arrival of the Spanish in the 1520s.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_17_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Population and political organization[MAJIDBENAM_BR]As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_18_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_19_MxAlb3B] Although the polity was politically fragmented, nobles continued to occupy hereditary positions of power, which were legitimated through their relationship with their ancestors.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_20_MxAlb3B] Nobles continued to have control over and exclusive access to resources and exercised rights not available to commoners “Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_21_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The overall population of the valley grew during this period, although people were no longer unified under one polity. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_22_MxAlb3B] The population of the whole valley has been estimated at 90,000-200,000 people. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_23_MxAlb3B]
♠ RA ♣ Alice Williams ♥
♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ suspected unknown ♥ As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_24_MxAlb3B]
♠ Polity Population ♣ [7245-9660] ♥ People. Gary Feinman (pers. comm.): population should be larger (>100,100? will get reference)[MAJIDBENAM_BR]The overall population of the valley increased during this period, but was divided into numerous (15-20, based on evidence for the MA V phase) smaller political entities.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_25_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]The population of the whole valley (based on the total of settlement population estimates) would have been 89,973-199,830 people.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_26_MxAlb3B] A very coarse estimate of the average polity population is taken as the average between the higher and lower population estimates for the whole valley (144,901 people) divided by 15 and 20 to give a higher and lower range of polity size (9660 and 7245 people respectively). The precise numbers for the polity population estimates should not be taken as accurate predictions of polity population size.[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Table 6.1. Late Classic population in Valley of Oaxaca subareas."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_27_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Etla: 24053 (MA IIIB); Central 39189 (MA IIIB); N Valle Grande 23000 (Early IIIB-IV); S Valle Grande: 13000 (Early IIIB-IV); W Tlacolula: 21000 (Early IIIB-IV); E Tlacolula: 18000 (Early IIIB-IV); Ejutla (Early IIIB-IV): 8000; Albarradas: 1000 (Early IIIB-IV); Sola: 7000 (Early IIIB-IV).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_28_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Total (Monte Alban IIIB): 154,242[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Table 6.1. Late Classic population in Valley of Oaxaca subareas."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_29_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Etla: 15000 (Late IIIB-IV); Central: 18000 (Late IIIB-IV); N Valle Grande: 18678 (MA IV); S Valle Grande: 9439 (MA IV); W Tlacolula: 15761 (MA IV); E Tlacolula: 24132 (MA IV); Ejutla: 3029 (MA IV); Albarradas: 2406 (MA IV); Sola: 7066 (MA IV).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_30_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Total (Monte Alban IV): 113,511[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Table 11.3. Population in the largest centers, by phase, in Oaxaca and Ejutla."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_31_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Valley of Oaxaca population (Largest center in Oaxaca): Tierras Largas: 327 (128); San Jose: 1942 (1384); Guadalupe: 1788 (774); Rosario: 1835 (564); Early I: 14652 (5250); Late I: 51339 (17242); Monte Alban II: 41927 (14492); Monte Alban IIIA: 120121 (16507); Monte Alban IIIB: 78930 (24189); Monte Alban IV: 77612 (16117); Monte Alban V: 166467 (13831).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_32_MxAlb3B]
♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ [15,000-30,000] ♥ Inhabitants. Monte Alban remained the largest settlement in the valley during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_33_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Population of at least 50,000 by Monte Alban IIIb (600-800 AD)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_34_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Table 11.3. Population in the largest centers, by phase, in Oaxaca and Ejutla."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_35_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Valley of Oaxaca population (Largest center in Oaxaca): Monte Alban IIIB: 78930 (24189); Monte Alban IV: 77612 (16117).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_36_MxAlb3B]

Hierarchical Complexity[edit]

♠ Settlement hierarchy ♣ 3: 500 CE-700 CE; 2: 700 CE-900 CE ♥ levels. Monte Alban declined in administrative importance over these periods, with Jalieza gradually becoming the largest settlement in the valley. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_37_MxAlb3B] Smaller, regional towns became more important in the subvalleys.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_38_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Inferred to be the IIIb phase (500-700 CE) settlement pattern:[MAJIDBENAM_REF_39_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]1. Primary centres-around 2 per cent of sites during this period had a population of 1000-2500 people. The larger sites had some evidence for administrative buildings, elite residences, plazas and occasional ballcourts[MAJIDBENAM_BR]2. Secondary centres-around 8 per cent of sites during this period had a population of minimum 180-500 to maximum 380-1000 people.
3. Smallest settlements-most sites during this period were small isolated residences, hamlets or villages of fewer than 290 people.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_40_MxAlb3B]
[MAJIDBENAM_BR]"Table 8.7. Monte Alban IV population hierarchy in Oaxaca and Ejuta."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_41_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] Valley of Oaxaca: Level 1: 16117; II: 5000-6222; III: 3590-4062; IV: 486-2900; V: 269-405; VI: 102-198; No rank: 8-95.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_42_MxAlb3B]
♠ Administrative levels ♣ ♥ levels. Later ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_43_MxAlb3B] However, it is by no means clear that the same system existed several centuries prior.
♠ Religious levels ♣ ♥ levels. Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the presence of full-time priests, or bigaña, during this period. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_44_MxAlb3B] The bigaña were ranked beneath the uija-táo (or great seer) and above the ueza-eche, huetete colanij (sacrifice or diviner).[MAJIDBENAM_REF_45_MxAlb3B] However, it is by no means clear that the same system existed several centuries prior.
♠ Military levels ♣ [1-2] ♥ levels. Later Spanish written records describe the presence of military officers and soldiers (civilian conscripts) during the MA V period, which may also apply to the MA IIIB-IV phases.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_46_MxAlb3B] However, it is by no means clear that the same system existed several centuries prior. Current code inferred from previous polities.

Professions[edit]

♠ Occupational complexity ♣ ♥
♠ Professional military officers ♣ inferred present ♥ Descriptions in the Spanish relaciones dating to the time of the Spanish conquest provide evidence for the presence of military officers in charge of competing armies at the end of this period. Their presence in the centuries before the Spanish conquest is inferred.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_47_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_48_MxAlb3B]
♠ Professional soldiers ♣ inferred present ♥ Inferred present from the descriptions of military officers and armies in the Spanish relaciones at the end of the subsequent MA V period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_49_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_50_MxAlb3B]
♠ Professional priesthood ♣ inferred present ♥ Although direct evidence for a priesthood is lacking for this period, their presence in the earlier Monte Alban phases and at the time of the Spanish conquest in the 1520s suggests that priests may continued to have been present during these intermediate periods.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_51_MxAlb3B]

Bureaucracy characteristics[edit]

♠ Full-time bureaucrats ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sixteenth-century Spanish written records refer to bureaucratic positions, such as the positions of tribute collector, ward boss and golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who collected goods and services from surrounding villages, and so bureaucratic positions may have been present in these earlier phases.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_52_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_53_MxAlb3B] However, we lack adequate information about administrative structures at Monte Albán to be able to discern whether full-time specialist bureaucrats (i.e. not just chiefs or generals with administrative duties) were present.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_54_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_55_MxAlb3B]
♠ Source of support ♣ suspected unknown ♥ There is little direct evidence for bureaucracy during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_56_MxAlb3B]
♠ Examination system ♣ suspected unknown ♥ There is little direct evidence for bureaucracy during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_57_MxAlb3B]
♠ Merit promotion ♣ suspected unknown ♥ There is little direct evidence for bureaucracy during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_58_MxAlb3B]
♠ Specialized government buildings ♣ inferred present: 500-800 CE; uncertain_present_absent: 800-900 CE ♥ There is little direct evidence for bureaucracy during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_59_MxAlb3B] However, Charles Spencer commented: 'One cannot fail to be impressed by the amount and variety of public/institutional architecture constructed between B.C. 300 and A.D. 800 in and around the Main Plaza at Monte Alban. The archaeological data indicate that much of this architectural complexity was in existence by Monte Alban II (100 B.C. through A.D. 200). Many of these buildings do not appear to have been residential and most likely served an array of religious (e.g., the various structures associated with two-room temples), military (such as Building J and perhaps the Ballcourt), and other "administrative" functions (including a variety of other public/institutional buildings the functions of which are still unclear). At the same time, it is probably also appropriate to consider the quihuitao (royal palace) to be another example of a public/institutional building. Both the main candidate for a quihuitao at Monte Alban (the Patio Hundido complex, according to Flannery) and also the example recently excavated at El Palenque (which was probably the capital of a rival independent state polity in Late Formative times) near San Martin Tilcajete (see Spencer and Redmond 2004 in Lat Am Antiq, and Redmond and Spencer 2017 PNAS) were not associated with tombs like other elite (and non-elite) residences; also, both examples had "residential" as well as "ceremonial/governmental" components. Note that the El Palenque quihuitao is securely dated to the Late Monte Albán I phase (300-100 B.C.)'.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_60_MxAlb3B]

Specialized Buildings: polity owned[edit]

♠ Communal buildings ♣ present ♥ Some of the larger (and middle) sites have evidence for plazas.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_65_MxAlb3B]
♠ Special purpose house ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for special purpose houses during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_66_MxAlb3B]
♠ Utilitarian public buildings ♣ present ♥ "Markets clearly have a long history in Mesoamerica before Aztec times. Back in the 1980s, I wrote a paper with Blanton and Kowalewski arguing that there were markets in Monte Albán I (ca. 500-200 BC). You can also find that argument in Ancient Mesoamerica and Ancient Oaxaca. While this may not yet be a consensual view yet, the literature on pre-Aztec markets across Mesoamerica is burgeoning."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_67_MxAlb3B]
♠ irrigation systems ♣ present ♥ Canals.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_68_MxAlb3B] Gary Feinman (pers. comm.) writes that small-scale irrigation, such as check-dams and small canals were in use. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_69_MxAlb3B]
♠ drinking water supply systems ♣ ♥
♠ markets ♣ present ♥ "Markets clearly have a long history in Mesoamerica before Aztec times. Back in the 1980s, I wrote a paper with Blanton and Kowalewski arguing that there were markets in Monte Albán I (ca. 500-200 BC). You can also find that argument in Ancient Mesoamerica and Ancient Oaxaca. While this may not yet be a consensual view yet, the literature on pre-Aztec markets across Mesoamerica is burgeoning."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_70_MxAlb3B] Feinman and Nicholas adopt a "multiscalar perspective" in their book chapter to argue that marketplaces were a central part of the economy during the Classic period in the Valley Oaxaca.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_71_MxAlb3B]
♠ food storage sites ♣ inferred absent ♥ No evidence for centralised food storage has been found at Monte Alban[MAJIDBENAM_REF_72_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_73_MxAlb3B] although smaller storage areas dating to the IV period have been found at the Guila Naquitz cave[MAJIDBENAM_REF_74_MxAlb3B] (these would presumably not have been state-owned).
♠ Symbolic buildings ♣ inferred absent ♥ Symbolic buildings are inferred absent as the monumental carvings of the time changed from being large displays of militaristic themes to smaller and portable depictions of life events of elites.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_75_MxAlb3B]
♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ present ♥ Some of the larger sites during this period have evidence for ballcourts.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_76_MxAlb3B]
♠ Knowledge/information buildings ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for specific knowledge or information buildings during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_77_MxAlb3B]
♠ Roads ♣ absent ♥ Only limited evidence for roads has been found (in earlier phases at Monte Alban), and these appear to have been restricted to within settlements. We asked Gary Feinman about roads in Oaxacan polities and he said: "It depends on what you mean by roads. There are definite roads/accessways within sites. Blanton defines some at Monte Albán and Linda [Nicholas] and I defined some at El Palmillo. These likely were not paved, but they may have been banked and were cleared. Between sites there are known 16th century trails, which were likely used for a long, long time. Again, they likely were not paved, but there were no beasts of burden."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_78_MxAlb3B] Coded absent: we do not count accessways within settlements or paths and trails not constructed deliberately as roads.
♠ Bridges ♣ inferred absent ♥ Sources do not suggest there is evidence for bridges in prehispanic Valley of Oaxaca.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_79_MxAlb3B]
♠ Canals ♣ inferred absent ♥ Although canals were present, they would not have been large enough to use as transport.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_80_MxAlb3B]
♠ Ports ♣ absent ♥ The Valley of Oaxaca is landlocked.

Information[edit]

♠ Length ♣ present ♥ The Zapotec unit of yaguen (26-27cm, or the length of a forearm) was used as a measure, and many stone monuments at Monte Alban are multiples of a yaguen.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_88_MxAlb3B]
♠ Area ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources do not suggest that any evidence has been found for units of measurement besides the yaguen for length.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_89_MxAlb3B]

♠ Mnemonic devices ♣ absent ♥ Genealogical registers of noble ancestry (including important marriages, and sometimes important life events of individuals) were recorded in stone during this period. Also carved glyphs denoting calendrical dates. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_95_MxAlb3B]
♠ Nonwritten records ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Written records ♣ present ♥ Genealogical registers of noble ancestry (including important marriages, and sometimes important life events of individuals) were recorded in stone during this period. Some examples have been found in tombs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_96_MxAlb3B] Mesoamerican civilizations, including the Maya, Aztec, Mixtec and Zapotec all possessed "a true form of writing: a series of hieroglyphs arranged in vertical columns and in many instances combined with numerals. The glyphs were at least indirectly related to a spoken language." Zapotec inscriptions are considered true writing, since the inscriptions had verbs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_97_MxAlb3B]
♠ Script ♣ present ♥ Evidence for carved glyphs and genealogical registers has been found.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_98_MxAlb3B]

Also carved glyphs denoting calendrical dates. Sources do not suggest that evidence for other types of writing has been found.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_100_MxAlb3B]

Military Technologies[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_122_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_123_MxAlb3B]
♠ Bronze ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_124_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_125_MxAlb3B]
♠ Iron ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_126_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_127_MxAlb3B]
♠ Steel ♣ absent ♥ Metalworking was not widely used in Mesoamerica, with metal products consisting mainly of small beads and ornaments.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_128_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_129_MxAlb3B]
♠ Javelins ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[MAJIDBENAM_REF_130_MxAlb3B] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include javelins. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.
♠ Other thrown weapons ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[MAJIDBENAM_REF_131_MxAlb3B] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include thrown weapons. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.
♠ Atlatl ♣ present ♥ Present in the valley of Oaxaca since preceramic times (the Proto-Otomangueans) for hunting.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_132_MxAlb3B] and, in previous periods, obsidian blades were found in Tomb 10 at San José Mogote which may have been hafted into atlatl darts.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_133_MxAlb3B] In addition, glyphs depicting what may be atlatls or spearthrowers have been carved with the danzantes at San Jose Mogote.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_134_MxAlb3B]
♠ Slings ♣ inferred present ♥ Sources[MAJIDBENAM_REF_135_MxAlb3B] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include slings. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias. Moreover, Spanish documents record the use of slings at the end of the Monte Alban V period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_136_MxAlb3B] David Carballo (pers. comm.) wrote that slings for projecting stone and ceramic slingshots were present. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_137_MxAlb3B]
♠ Self bow ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[MAJIDBENAM_REF_138_MxAlb3B] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include bows of any kind. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias. Moreover, Spanish documents record the use of bows and arrows at the end of the Monte Alban V period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_139_MxAlb3B]
♠ Composite bow ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[MAJIDBENAM_REF_140_MxAlb3B] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include bows of any kind. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias. Moreover, Spanish documents record the use of bows and arrows at the end of the Monte Alban V period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_141_MxAlb3B]
♠ Crossbow ♣ absent ♥ Hassig lists crossbows among the new military technologies the Spanish introduced to the region in the sixteenth century[MAJIDBENAM_REF_142_MxAlb3B]

♠ Sling siege engines ♣ absent ♥

♠ Daggers ♣ disputed_present_absent ♥ Obsidian-edged wooden swords and daggers are inferred present based the presence of obsidian blades in the valley.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_146_MxAlb3B] David Carballo (pers. comm.) wrote that these obsidian blades were knives rather than swords or daggers. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_147_MxAlb3B]
♠ Swords ♣ disputed_present_absent ♥ Obsidian-edged wooden swords and daggers are inferred present based the presence of obsidian blades in the valley.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_148_MxAlb3B] David Carballo (pers. comm.) wrote that these obsidian blades were knives rather than swords or daggers. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_149_MxAlb3B]
♠ Spears ♣ inferred present ♥ Present in the valley of Oaxaca since preceramic times (the Proto-Otomangueans) for hunting.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_150_MxAlb3B] However, it does seem to be clear whether they were also used as weapons in warfare.
♠ Polearms ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Sources[MAJIDBENAM_REF_151_MxAlb3B] only mention very little archaeological evidence for weaponry for this period, and this does not include polearms. However, weapons made from wood and cloth have been documented for the later periods, so their absence in the archaeological record may be due to preservation bias.

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ present ♥ Monte Albán was built on a hill 400m above the valley floor and a number of other settlements were located on hilltops.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_168_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_169_MxAlb3B]

♠ Skeletal evidence of violence ♣ absent ♥ Not mentioned by sources.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_217_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Did the Zapotecs experience similar conditions to the Maya at this time? "Over the course of three centuries, from 750 to 1050 CE a succession of serious and prolonged droughts, previously inferred by archaeologists, occurred. They undermined food yields and social cohesion in the Maya; starvation and local conflict (evident in skeletal remains), social unrest and ultimately violent uprisings unseated the rulers of many of the great centres."[MAJIDBENAM_REF_218_MxAlb3B]
♠ Mass burials ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned by sources.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_219_MxAlb3B]
♠ Settlement destruction ♣ present ♥ A few sites show evidence for burned daub (common buildings material)[MAJIDBENAM_REF_220_MxAlb3B] so it is here assumed that settlement destruction was less prevalent than in earlier phases.
♠ Pictorial evidence of warfare ♣ inferred absent ♥ Carvings from these periods mainly depict marriages and refer to genealogical registers rather than military scenes.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_221_MxAlb3B]

With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Code the variables below for the collective ritual of the official cult that is performed on the largest scale. Largest scale refers to the largest number of people a ritual brings together in a single location. If there are several rituals that have the same level of scale, code the most frequent one.
♠ Name ♣ Temple dedications ♥ Monte Alban is where the evidence for temple dedications becomes apparent. Several caches have been found under corners of temples. Objects include pottery, necklace of shell, mother of pearl mosaic. Skeletons also found at one site, Temple H, they were mostly young adults and had substantial amounts of jewellery-jade necklaces, earrings and masks.[MAJIDBENAM_BR][MAJIDBENAM_REF_230_MxAlb3B]

Most widespread collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Code the variables below for the most widespread collective ritual of the official cult. Widespread refers to the degree of coverage a ritual has in a polity and therefore to the proportion of the overall population that participates in or observes a ritual. Keep in mind that it is possible that the ritual with the highest coverage is not necessarily a mass ritual performed at one location, but can be a ritual that is performed all over the polity and of which the aggregate of the audiences represent the largest degree of coverage.

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ ballgame ♥ "The quantity of ceramic figurined depicting ballplayers throughout Mesoamerica brings home Susanna Ekholm's (1991) point that it was a wildly popular pastime with the commoners and was likely played by both women and men." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_239_MxAlb3B] Though not much has been written about how the ballgame was performed in Oaxaca specifically, accounts and reconstructions relating to other parts of Mesoamerica suggest that this was a euphoric ritual. "John G. Fox (1996) has made the important point that a great deal of feasting took place in association with the games and the courts on which they were played. He suggests that feasting fulfilled significant connective functions, both within and between communities of all size. The games staged by Aztec kings and nobles often ended with music and dance (Duran 1967:206; Sahagun 1950-1982, 2:299)." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_240_MxAlb3B]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Duration ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ [2000-5000] ♥ "The scale, accessibility, openness, and symbolism of the Main Plaza indicate that it was constructed as an arena where thousands of people could have participated in public rituals." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_241_MxAlb3B]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ "Nobles and especially rulers, however, had a greater degree of control over people’s access to the divine, since there is little evidence for non-elite domestic rituals other than mortuary ceremonies (Winter 2002:81- 2; Winter et al. 2007:192). Prominent nobles and rulers had access to ritual buildings and spaces and filled certain religious offices, like that of Xicani priest, that gave them special access to the divine." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_242_MxAlb3B]
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The quantity of ceramic figurined depicting ballplayers throughout Mesoamerica brings home Susanna Ekholm's (1991) point that it was a wildly popular pastime with the commoners and was likely played by both women and men. Early Formative figurines documented by Douglas Bradley (2001) clearly attest to the presence of female ballplayers. Fray Bartolome de las Casas (1967:350) saw games played by women, possibly at Hispaniola. Classic Maya reliefs at Yaxchilan show royal women with a ball in one hand, indicating they indeed played the game or at the very least put the ball into play." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_243_MxAlb3B]
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ whole polity ♥ "Until the Nisa phase, the Main Plaza was open on its eastern side, making activities on the plaza accessible to commoners living on the terraces below." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_244_MxAlb3B] "The size, accessibility, and symbolism of the Main Plaza suggest to me that during Monte Albán’s first four centuries the plaza was a focus of public ceremonies participated in by people of varied identities including different statuses, corporate groups, and communities." ♠ honour code ♣ disputed_absent_present ♥ "The evidence suggests to me that commoners were less actively engaged in the kinds of ritual performances and shared experiences that created a sense of belonging and identity with the symbols, rulers, and institutions of the polity. [...] The Main Plaza may have embodied an increasing separation, and perhaps tension, between the identity of commoners and rulers as well as between people in the center and periphery. Residences were increasingly enclosed and inwardly focused during the Late Classic, which might reflect these rising social tensions and divisions, especially among competing nobles and/or between nobles and commoners (Hutson 2002:68- 9; Winter 1974). An unintended outcome of competition among the nobility and the disengagement of commoners from public ceremonies could have been to weaken the allegiance of commoners and lesser nobles to rulers and ruling institutions, especially to the distant rulers of Monte Albán. It is possible that for many people the Main Plaza and other restricted ceremonial spaces came to symbolize the evasion of the moral responsibilities of rulers to their people." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_254_MxAlb3B] "John G. Fox (1996) has made the important point that a great deal of feasting took place in association with the games and the courts on which they were played. He suggests that feasting fulfilled significant connective functions, both within and between communities of all size." ♠ currency ♣ absent ♥ No evidence for currency use at this time.
♠ property/valuable items ♣ inferred present ♥ Among the Aztecs, "[b]etting formed a major aspect of the sport, and both spectators and players gambled heavily on the outcome of this ritual game of chance. The nobility never seemed to lack the wealth oto pay their gambling debts, but addictive gambling by people of low status could bring disaster. In an early 16th-century chronicle, Fray Diego Duran reports: 'They ... gambled their homes, their corn granaries, their maguey plants. They sold their children in order to bet and even staked themselves and became slaves to be sacrificed later if they were not ransomed." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_264_MxAlb3B]
♠ health costs ♣ inferred present ♥ Among the Aztecs, "[b]lows from the heavy rubber ball could leave them badly injured, or even dead, but the dangers apparently paled in comparison to the glory enjoyed by the greatest heroic athletes." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_265_MxAlb3B]

♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ inferred present ♥ "Numerous scenes of human sacrifice in post-game rituals are depicted on ballcourt reliefs[...]. It seems clear that by staging games that resulted in sacrifices, the rulers of ancient Mesoamerican towns were intent on demonstrating their power over life and death. This power was clearly exercised on both their enemies and their own subjects." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_268_MxAlb3B]

Most dysphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

Unlike the preceding rituals, the most dysphoric collective ritual may not belong to the official cult, but may be practiced by another group that has a significant role in the polity.
♠ Name ♣ ♥ In the narrative part, also give a brief description of the ritual
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ ♥ daily/weekly/monthly/yearly/once per generation/once in a lifetime. Code the frequency of participating in a ritual from the perspective of the ritual specialist (e.g. priest).
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ ♥ daily/weekly/monthly/yearly/once per generation/once in a lifetime. Code the frequency of participating in a ritual from the perspective of the participants. This group of participants does not include the ritual specialists, like priests. Highly engaged audiences (audiences who are processing, jeering, ...) are considered as participants. Audiences consisting of passive spectators or witnesses are coded for in the next variable. When it is difficult to distinguish between a highly engaged audience and an audience consisting of passive spectators, code these audiences here.
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ ♥ daily/weekly/monthly/yearly/once per generation/once in a lifetime. Code the frequency of participating in a ritual from the perspective of a typical member of an audience consisting of passive spectators or witnesses.
♠ Duration ♣ ♥ Indicate the duration of the ritual, including the unit of measurement (e.g. 2 hours or 3 days). In case the duration of the ritual varies (e.g. the duration varies between 6 and 10 hours) code for the midpoint (i.e. 8 hours).
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ ♥ in absolute numbers
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ ♥ Ritual specialists comprise individuals coming from which of the following groups: whole polity/ all adult males/ ruling elites/ dominant ethnic group/ dominant military unit/ another kind of interest group (specify)
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ ♥ Participants comprise individuals coming from which of the following groups: whole polity/ all adult males/ ruling elites/ dominant ethnic group/ dominant military unit/ another kind of interest group (specify)

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Gréine Jordan ♥

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of rulers the relationship between rulers and the military is unclear. “With the decline of Monte Albán around A.D. 700, the Zapotec entered into their fourth developmental stage: a period of highly competitive and militaristic “city states” which lasted until the Spanish conquest of the 1520s.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_270_MxAlb3B] As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_271_MxAlb3B] "Nobles were distinguished from commoners by dress, access to exotic goods, control of esoteric knowledge (e.g., special rituals, literacy), participation in restricted rituals, and by residing in architecturally elaborate houses. Perhaps nowhere is the special social position of nobility as clearly portrayed as in imagery on carved-stone monuments and tomb murals. Nobles are shown in elaborate attire, sometimes dressed as jaguars, richly ornamented and often shown in the act of dominating captives, performing human sacrifice, marrying, or communicating with ancestors." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_272_MxAlb3B] "To summarize, imagery on tomb art and carved-stone monuments shows how Classic-period political authority was more exclusionary and less communal than during the Formative (A. Joyce 2004:207- 11; Urcid 2005:154- 5). Genealogy became a key element of ideologies that legitimated the social position of corporate groups as well as rulers. Using Giddens’ (1979:193- 5) framework for understanding how ideology creates, maintains, and justifies sectional interests, genealogy can be seen as having reified status distinctions among corporate groups since the prominence of apical ancestors was fixed. The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_273_MxAlb3B]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of rulers the relationship between rulers and any possible legislative is unclear. “With the decline of Monte Albán around A.D. 700, the Zapotec entered into their fourth developmental stage: a period of highly competitive and militaristic “city states” which lasted until the Spanish conquest of the 1520s.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_274_MxAlb3B] As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_275_MxAlb3B] "Nobles were distinguished from commoners by dress, access to exotic goods, control of esoteric knowledge (e.g., special rituals, literacy), participation in restricted rituals, and by residing in architecturally elaborate houses. Nobles are shown in elaborate attire, sometimes dressed as jaguars, richly ornamented and often shown in the act of dominating captives, performing human sacrifice, marrying, or communicating with ancestors." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_276_MxAlb3B] "To summarize, imagery on tomb art and carved-stone monuments shows how Classic-period political authority was more exclusionary and less communal than during the Formative (A. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_281_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR] ♠ Right to petition executive ♣ suspected unknown ♥ While there is evidence of rulers the relationship between rulers and subjects/government/elites/religious agents is unclear. There is no evidence for a formal legal system during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_282_MxAlb3B] imagery on tomb art and carved-stone monuments shows how Classic-period political authority was more exclusionary and less communal than during the Formative (A. Joyce 2004:207- 11; Urcid 2005:154- 5). Genealogy became a key element of ideologies that legitimated the social position of corporate groups as well as rulers. Using Giddens’ (1979:193- 5) framework for understanding how ideology creates, maintains, and justifies sectional interests, genealogy can be seen as having reified status distinctions among corporate groups since the prominence of apical ancestors was fixed. The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_283_MxAlb3B]

Perhaps nowhere is the special social position of nobility as clearly portrayed as in imagery on carved-stone monuments and tomb murals. Nobles are shown in elaborate attire, sometimes dressed as jaguars, richly ornamented and often shown in the act of dominating captives, performing human sacrifice, marrying, or communicating with ancestors." Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: There is little direct evidence for bureaucracy during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_328_MxAlb3B] evidence from the subsequent MA V period. Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_329_MxAlb3B] Although Spanish written records refer to bureaucratic positions, such as the positions of tribute collector, ward boss and golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who collected goods and services from surrounding villages, and so bureaucratic positions may have been present in these earlier phases.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_330_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_331_MxAlb3B] From Phase 1: “With the decline of Monte Albán around A.D. 700, the Zapotec entered into their fourth developmental stage: a period of highly competitive and militaristic “city states” which lasted until the Spanish conquest of the 1520s.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_332_MxAlb3B] As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_333_MxAlb3B]

Property Rights[edit]

♠ Private property (land) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_541_MxAlb3B]

♠ patrilineal inheritance ♣ inferred present ♥ From Religion: "Gender was represented in elements of clothing with males often shown wearing short kilts or loincloths and women attired in blouses and long skirts. Ruling nobles were usually men dressed as jaguars with feline heads covering their faces and with clawed extremities over their hands and feet. These Zapotec kings were often shown paired with queens. Other figures depicted in the tombs were probably collateral kin related to the dynastic line who may have led communities subject to the ruling couple. The imagery suggests that descent was reckoned through the male line, although there were instances where powerful women figured prominently in the succession of rulers." Sex Class and Race[edit]

Marriage
♠ Monogamy ♣ inferred absent ♥ From Religion: "High-status residences often included burials of adult females with elaborate offerings that Urcid (2005:40- 1) interprets as the secondary wives of male household heads, which is consistent with evidence for polygyny among nobles at the time of the Spanish Conquest." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_560_MxAlb3B] The Zapotec state began to fragment at the end of the IIIA period, and eventually formed numerous smaller competing "kingdoms", each politically independent of the others[MAJIDBENAM_REF_561_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_562_MxAlb3B] although there were marriage alliances between elites, as recorded in genealogical registers. Marriage alliances became increasingly important as a political tool through the IIIB to IV periods.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_563_MxAlb3B]
♠ Formal Elite Polygamy ♣ inferred present ♥ From Religion: "High-status residences often included burials of adult females with elaborate offerings that Urcid (2005:40- 1) interprets as the secondary wives of male household heads, which is consistent with evidence for polygyny among nobles at the time of the Spanish Conquest." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_564_MxAlb3B] The Zapotec state began to fragment at the end of the IIIA period, and eventually formed numerous smaller competing "kingdoms", each politically independent of the others[MAJIDBENAM_REF_565_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_566_MxAlb3B] although there were marriage alliances between elites, as recorded in genealogical registers. Marriage alliances became increasingly important as a political tool through the IIIB to IV periods.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_567_MxAlb3B]

♠ Ethnicity or race ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Religion: It is worth noting that the "Oaxaca enclave at Teotihuacan maintained a distinctive identity for over five centuries despite the fact that many of the enclave residents were born in Teotihuacan and spent much of (p. 678) their adult lives there (Spence et al. 2005)." At the same time, it seems that, in Mesoamerica generally, one's ethnicity was not necessarily fixed: "across their lives and in different social situations, people could have variably deployed or displayed their class or ethnic positions." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_570_MxAlb3B]

♠ Education is available to both males and females ♣ suspected unknown ♥ education system is unknown
♠ Property can be owned by both males and females ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: Genealogy became a key element of ideologies that legitimated the social position of corporate groups as well as rulers. Using Giddens’ (1979:193- 5) framework for understanding how ideology creates, maintains, and justifies sectional interests, genealogy can be seen as having reified status distinctions among corporate groups since the prominence of apical ancestors was fixed. The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_583_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Discrimination on the basis of social class (include de facto limitations even when there are no explicit legal restrictions)

The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_596_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_BR]Discrimination on the basis of ethnicity/race
♠ Bureaucratic positions are open to people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: There is little direct evidence for bureaucracy during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_597_MxAlb3B] evidence from the subsequent MA V period. Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_598_MxAlb3B] Although Spanish written records refer to bureaucratic positions, such as the positions of tribute collector, ward boss and golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who collected goods and services from surrounding villages, and so bureaucratic positions may have been present in these earlier phases.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_599_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_600_MxAlb3B] From Phase 1: “With the decline of Monte Albán around A.D. 700, the Zapotec entered into their fourth developmental stage: a period of highly competitive and militaristic “city states” which lasted until the Spanish conquest of the 1520s.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_601_MxAlb3B] As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_602_MxAlb3B]

♠ Property can be owned by people from all ethnic/racial groups ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: Genealogy became a key element of ideologies that legitimated the social position of corporate groups as well as rulers. Using Giddens’ (1979:193- 5) framework for understanding how ideology creates, maintains, and justifies sectional interests, genealogy can be seen as having reified status distinctions among corporate groups since the prominence of apical ancestors was fixed. The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_609_MxAlb3B]

Status[edit]

Elite status
♠ elite status is hereditary ♣ present ♥ From Phase 1: Genealogical registers of noble ancestry (including important marriages, and sometimes important life events of individuals) were recorded in stone during this period. Some examples have been found in tombs.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_610_MxAlb3B]"To summarize, imagery on tomb art and carved-stone monuments shows how Classic-period political authority was more exclusionary and less communal than during the Formative (A. Joyce 2004:207- 11; Urcid 2005:154- 5). Genealogy became a key element of ideologies that legitimated the social position of corporate groups as well as rulers. Using Giddens’ (1979:193- 5) framework for understanding how ideology creates, maintains, and justifies sectional interests, genealogy can be seen as having reified status distinctions among corporate groups since the prominence of apical ancestors was fixed. The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_611_MxAlb3B]

Deification of Rulers[edit]

♠ Rulers are legitimated by gods ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Iconography suggests that rulers and other elites presented themselves as conduits to the realm of the gods, intermediaries between humans and gods. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_642_MxAlb3B]
♠ Rulers control supernatural energy ♣ present ♥ "Important nobles were depicted as intermediaries between common people and the divine, especially through the ability of the former to contact their deified ancestors." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_643_MxAlb3B]
♠ Rulers are descendants of gods ♣ present ♥ "The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_644_MxAlb3B] Iconography suggests that rulers and other elites presented themselves as conduits to the realm of the gods, intermediaries between humans and gods. [MAJIDBENAM_REF_645_MxAlb3B]
♠ Rulers become gods after death ♣ present ♥ "During the Classic period, after A.D. 200, urns of venerated royal ancestors often show them wearing a mask of Cociyo to indicate their association with sacred lightning (Fig. 9.7). Other royal ancestors who had their names linked to powerful supernatural forces include Xoo, 'Earthquake,' and Piy, 'Wind' (Marcus 1983h, 1983p).//Associated with lightning were elements such as clouds, wind, rain, and hail. In fact, the Zapotec believed that royal ancestors ascended to heaven and became clouds, sometimes depicted as flying turtles. Their heavenly position allowed them to intercede with lightning on behalf of their descendants. They would only do so, however, if properly cared for and appropriately propitiated with sacrificed by their descendants." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_646_MxAlb3B]

Normative Ideological Aspects of Equity and Prosociality[edit]

♠ Ideological reinforcement of equality ♣ absent ♥ "Nobles were distinguished from commoners by dress, access to exotic goods, control of esoteric knowledge (e.g., special rituals, literacy), participation in restricted rituals, and by residing in architecturally elaborate houses. Perhaps nowhere is the special social position of nobility as clearly portrayed as in imagery on carved-stone monuments and tomb murals. Nobles are shown in elaborate attire, sometimes dressed as jaguars, richly ornamented and often shown in the act of dominating captives, performing human sacrifice, marrying, or communicating with ancestors." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_649_MxAlb3B] "To summarize, imagery on tomb art and carved-stone monuments shows how Classic-period political authority was more exclusionary and less communal than during the Formative (A. Joyce 2004:207- 11; Urcid 2005:154- 5). Genealogy became a key element of ideologies that legitimated the social position of corporate groups as well as rulers. Using Giddens’ (1979:193- 5) framework for understanding how ideology creates, maintains, and justifies sectional interests, genealogy can be seen as having reified status distinctions among corporate groups since the prominence of apical ancestors was fixed. The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_650_MxAlb3B]
♠ Ideological thought equates rulers and commoners ♣ absent ♥ "Nobles were distinguished from commoners by dress, access to exotic goods, control of esoteric knowledge (e.g., special rituals, literacy), participation in restricted rituals, and by residing in architecturally elaborate houses. Nobles are shown in elaborate attire, sometimes dressed as jaguars, richly ornamented and often shown in the act of dominating captives, performing human sacrifice, marrying, or communicating with ancestors." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_653_MxAlb3B] "To summarize, imagery on tomb art and carved-stone monuments shows how Classic-period political authority was more exclusionary and less communal than during the Formative (A. Joyce 2004:207- 11; Urcid 2005:154- 5). Genealogy became a key element of ideologies that legitimated the social position of corporate groups as well as rulers. Using Giddens’ (1979:193- 5) framework for understanding how ideology creates, maintains, and justifies sectional interests, genealogy can be seen as having reified status distinctions among corporate groups since the prominence of apical ancestors was fixed. The imagery presented apical ancestors of noble groups as instrumental in the cosmic creation and their continued propitiation was crucial to world-centering rituals. This belief universalized the social position of the nobility by making nobles intermediaries between commoners and the divine, since nobles were both descended from and had privileged access to important ancestors. Wealth and status was further reified since rights to property, privileges, and special offices were held via membership in the corporate group. Members of ruling houses, for example, had the right to engage in warfare, take captives, play the ballgame, and offer human sacrifices. Status within the group was further reified as a function of a person’s proximity to the apical ancestor. Indications that important offices could be inherited, such as that of rainmaker and paramount sacrificer, suggest the means through which other nobles as well as commoners could be excluded from positions of power." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_654_MxAlb3B]
♠ Ideological thought equates all ethnicities ♣ suspected unknown ♥ It is worth noting that the "Oaxaca enclave at Teotihuacan maintained a distinctive identity for over five centuries despite the fact that many of the enclave residents were born in Teotihuacan and spent much of (p. 678) their adult lives there (Spence et al. 2005)." At the same time, it seems that, in Mesoamerica generally, one's ethnicity was not necessarily fixed: "across their lives and in different social situations, people could have variably deployed or displayed their class or ethnic positions." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_655_MxAlb3B]
♠ Ideological thought equates men and women ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Gender was represented in elements of clothing with males often shown wearing short kilts or loincloths and women attired in blouses and long skirts. Ruling nobles were usually men dressed as jaguars with feline heads covering their faces and with clawed extremities over their hands and feet. These Zapotec kings were often shown paired with queens. Other figures depicted in the tombs were probably collateral kin related to the dynastic line who may have led communities subject to the ruling couple. The imagery suggests that descent was reckoned through the male line, although there were instances where powerful women figured prominently in the succession of rulers." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_656_MxAlb3B]
♠ Ideological thought equates people of all locations ♣ suspected unknown ♥ absent/present/unknown. People are seen as equal regardless of where they are from (regardless of whether other equalities are also observed, for instance gender)

Normative Ideological Aspects of Human Sacrifice[edit]

♠ Human sacrifice ♣ [2-5] ♥ "Nobles were distinguished from commoners by dress, access to exotic goods, control of esoteric knowledge (e.g., special rituals, literacy), participation in restricted rituals, and by residing in architecturally elaborate houses. Perhaps nowhere is the special social position of nobility as clearly portrayed as in imagery on carved-stone monuments and tomb murals. Nobles are shown in elaborate attire, sometimes dressed as jaguars, richly ornamented and often shown in the act of dominating captives, performing human sacrifice, marrying, or communicating with ancestors." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_666_MxAlb3B] "Interments of rulers may have at times included human sacrifice." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_667_MxAlb3B]
♠ human sacrifice of an out-group member ♣ present ♥ "Noble identity and ideology was inscribed in the iconography and epigraphy of carved stones, slabs, and lintels (Moser 1977; Rivera 1999, 2000). [...] Common themes include (figure 7.11): [...] scenes depicting conquest and human sacrifice. These scenes often show a human figure inclined over a hill glyph with a sign representing a particular place such as the 'Hill of the Lizard.' Adjacent to the figure is a calendrical name of a ruler with a human hand holding a weapon, which extends over the inclined figure, representing the conquest of the identified place and probably the sacrifice of a ruler and/or war captive." [MAJIDBENAM_REF_668_MxAlb3B]

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ suspected unknown ♥ From Phase 1: There is little direct evidence for bureaucracy during this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_714_MxAlb3B] evidence from the subsequent MA V period. Ethnohistoric records written by the Spanish after 1520 describe the coqui or noblemen who oversaw the villages, as well as the golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who oversaw the collection of goods and services from the villages.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_715_MxAlb3B] Although Spanish written records refer to bureaucratic positions, such as the positions of tribute collector, ward boss and golaba, or “lord’s solicitor” who collected goods and services from surrounding villages, and so bureaucratic positions may have been present in these earlier phases.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_716_MxAlb3B][MAJIDBENAM_REF_717_MxAlb3B] From Phase 1: “With the decline of Monte Albán around A.D. 700, the Zapotec entered into their fourth developmental stage: a period of highly competitive and militaristic “city states” which lasted until the Spanish conquest of the 1520s.”[MAJIDBENAM_REF_718_MxAlb3B] As the Zapotec state was in a process of fragmentation into smaller polities, the actual extent of any polity in the Valley of Oaxaca is very difficult to determine for this period.[MAJIDBENAM_REF_719_MxAlb3B]
